<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="1c65303e618e46cfa47a906e633bf68c"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1c65303e618e46cfa47a906e633bf68c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ffe75211-067d-4a8d-9d32-d035e8ae828e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d235616885" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Anode-free sodium metal batteries represent great promising as high-energy-density and resource-rich electrochemical energy storage systems. However, the savage growth of sodium metal and continuous consumption hinder its stable capacity output. Herein, ordered flower-edges of zinc on Al substrate can induce high-entropy solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) to adjust sodium uniform deposition and extremely reduce electrolyte consumption with ultrahigh initial Coulombic efficiency (97.05%) for prolong batteries cycling life. Theoretical and experimental studies have demonstrated that the electron-donating property and exposed edge sites between (100) and (101) facets in zinc flower enhance anion adsorption onto the inner Helmholtz plane accelerating its interface decomposition. Additionally, the ordered zinc edges serve as homogeneous-nucleating template, leading to thin and inorganic-rich SEI layer (18 nm, ZnF<sub>2</sub>, NaZn<sub>13</sub>, NaF, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) with high-entropy discrete multicomponent distribution, so that fast and high-flux Na ions transport field, thereby reducing the critical nucleation barrier and promoting sodium high density nucleation (7.36 × 10<sup>13</sup> N cm<sup>−2</sup>) and pyknotic growth (3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, 22&nbsp;µm). The assembled anode-free sodium batteries exhibit high stability (86%, 90 cycles) under ultrahigh cathode loading (32 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>). Moreover, the anode-less single-layer pouch batteries exhibit a durable capacity retention of 99% after 600 cycles.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202413253-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202413253-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium (Na)-based batteries are regarded as one of the most promising grid-scale energy storage systems because of their extremely low price advantage and wide geographical distribution of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0001_R_d235616717e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater. Chem. Rev.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, employing Na metal with a low operating potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode) and high specific capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) as anodes demonstrate a higher energy density than close-commercial hard carbon anodes (300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0002_R_d235616717e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the utilization of Na anodes can result in certain undesirable issues associated with the high reactivity and uncontrolled growth of dendrites during repeated plating/stripping processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0003_R_d235616717e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the larger N/P ratio hinders the realization of high energy density for sodium metal batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0004_R_d235616717e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, processing soft and adhesive metallic sodium into ultrathin layers is challenging, potentially compromising the energy density due to excess sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0005_R_d235616717e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, anode-free sodium metal battery (AFSMB) configurations that eliminate the use of any sodium metal in the battery production process offer remarkable advantages in terms of energy density, cost-effectiveness, and ease of production; thus, they serve as ideal alternatives.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0006_R_d235616717e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Nat. Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, during the operation of AFSMBs, the uneven deposition morphology and interfacial parasitic reactions can lead to the generation of dead “Na” and electrolyte consumption, resulting in active Na consumption and a rapid decline in capacity.</p>
         
         <p>The structure and properties of the substrate interface and electrolyte are widely acknowledged as crucial factors for the formation of an SEI and the evolution of sodium deposition, which affects the life of anode-free batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0007_R_d235616717e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Mater. Natl. Sci. Rev.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In recent years, significant efforts have been dedicated to achieving uniform sodium deposition through various means, such as modifying the interface<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and optimizing electrolyte formulas.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0009_R_d235616717e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With respect to interface modification, sodiophilic metal coatings/sites (e.g., Cu, Ag, Fe, Sn, and Zn) have usually been introduced to control the nucleation and growth of sodium, thus improving the cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0005_R_d235616717e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a>, <a href="#adma202413253-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0010_R_d235616717e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Sci. China Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Rui et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> constructed a 3D nanofiber-like Zn layer on Al foil, which enhances the sodiophilicity of the Zn layer and homogenizes the electric field distribution to suppress the dendritic Na growth and improve the reversibility of Na plating/stripping at 2 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. However, the primary obstacle to the implementation of metal coatings in high areal capacity batteries lies in the deactivation of sodiophilic sites because sodium metal covers the sodiophilic sites. Wang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designed a fluorinated porous triazine framework on a Cu substrate to induce a stable NaF-dominated SEI and reduce the reactivity of Na<sup>+</sup> for inevitable Na<sup>+</sup> depletion by well-defined and adjustable F substituent groups, which exhibited excellent cycling performance with 400 cycles at 0.74 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. In terms of electrolyte optimization, Guo et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0009_R_d235616717e620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have demonstrated that refining the volume ratio of two conventional linear ether solvents facilitates the formation of NaF-rich SEI on Al-C substrates to guide dendrite-free planar epitaxial metal growth over 500&nbsp;h at 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and improve cycling stability (50 cycles). The relevant studies have demonstrated that NaF-enriched SEI can passivate sodium metal, reduce electrolyte consumption, improve the density of Na deposition, and extend the battery operating life. However, the effectiveness of the aforementioned SEI is not satisfactory for high areal capacity (≥3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a>, <a href="#adma202413253-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0011_R_d235616717e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The high-entropy concept has recently been applied to the solid electrolyte and material design on the basis of the principle that increasing compositional diversity can lead to unexpected ion transport flux and stability properties that cannot be achieved with individual or binary components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0012_R_d235616717e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Science Nat. Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by this, it is hypothesize that incorporating various inorganic species into the SEI could effectively improve the density of sodium deposits and the cycling stability of AFSMBs at high areal capacity. Therefore, a high-entropy SEI was rationally constructed with a multicomponent discrete distribution by introducing an edge-enriched and highly ordered zinc flower matrix on the surface of Al substrate, which could adjust Na deposition and reduce the electrolyte consumption with a high cathode loading. Flower-like zinc not only acts as a sodiophilic seeding/host but also participates in the crystallization and growth of interfacial phases. The flower-like zinc structure possesses abundant ordered exposed edge sites between the (100) and (101) facets as perfect catalytic and hosting sites for facilitating the anion aggregation to enter the inner Helmholtz plane and decompose there. As a result, a thin and inorganic-rich SEI layer with a high-entropy discrete multicomponent distribution was acquired, and the highly compact and reversible Na deposition was also achieved under high areal capacity.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202413253-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202413253-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The conventional industrial electroless plating strategy was applied to achieve a homogeneous zinc flower matrix with a uniform size and enhanced edge coverage on the Al substrate via a roll-to-roll electroless plating process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information); details in the Experimental Section). The structure and the coverage of zinc on the Al substrate surface can be easily controlled by adjusting the electroless plating time. The optimal electroless plating time of the substrate is determined by the electrochemical performance of the asymmetric batteries, namely, the nucleation overpotential (NOP), voltage hysteresis (VH), initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), average Coulombic efficiency (ACE), and cycle life (CL) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The values of NOP and VH for the Na metal batteries of the substrates with different plating times (Zn@Al-X, X&nbsp;= 0, 5, 10, 15, 20, 25, and 30 s) are −130/25, −115/30, −35/28, −35/30, −15/20, −45/35, and −40/25&nbsp;mV, respectively. A lower NOP and VH indicate a greater availability of sodium plating sites and enhanced system compatibility for uniform Na nucleation and growth. The ICE, CL, and ACE of Zn@Al-20 s are 98.6%, 3000&nbsp;h and 99.94%, respectively, which are much higher than those of the substrates with other plating times. Scanning electron microscope (SEM) was employed to observe the morphology of zinc at different electroless plating times (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1a</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The initially formed grains were scattered hexagonal prisms (5 s). With the extension of reaction time to 15 s, the coverage area of zinc increased and the edges of the hexagonal prism gradually became thinner and stratified. When the reaction time reached 20 s, uniform and well-ordered zinc flowers with rich-edges were observed. At 30 s, there was a significant stacking and aggregation of zinc flowers. Therefore, an Al substrate with 20 s of electroless plating, abbreviated as E-Zn@Al, was chosen for further study. SEM, energy-dispersive spectroscopy, and atomic force microscopy (AFM) images revealed that zinc with an edge-enriched nanosheet crosslinking structure was uniformly distributed on both sides of the Al substrate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information); and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1a</a>). The optical photograph depicts a tangible representation of the extensive fabrication process for E-Zn@Al substrates (illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1a</a>). The cross-sectional images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) show that the thickness of the zinc matrix is ≈2&nbsp;µm. The highly ordered porous structure could promote electrolyte infiltration and ion transport (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The flower edges are nearly hexagonal with well-defined facets. The lattice-resolved image shows lattice fringes with spacings of 0.23 and 0.21&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (100) and (101) lattice planes of Zn, respectively. This finding indicates that the zinc-modified layer edge consists of four (101), two (100), and two (002) facets (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1b,c</a>), well-defined crystal facets are beneficial for the adsorption of anions. Furthermore, the Rietveld refinement of XRD results revealed that the Zn flowers presented lattice parameters of a = b = 2.67 Å, c = 4.95 Å, α = γ = 90° and β = 120° with a space group of P63/mmc.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202413253-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/87506ac3-98b0-4cec-947d-7fdf17e6c929/adma202413253-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/87506ac3-98b0-4cec-947d-7fdf17e6c929/adma202413253-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9e92f63f-4040-44c7-b8fa-b13e97bf1cec/adma202413253-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/87506ac3-98b0-4cec-947d-7fdf17e6c929/adma202413253-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413253-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The morphology and electron properties of zinc flower edges. a) SEM images, illustrated as optical images. b,c) TEM images. The well-defined crystal facets of the edge site. d) XRD Rietveld refinement. e,f) Kelvin probe force microscopy images of the interfacial potentials of the electrodes of the Al and E-Zn@Al substrates. g) Mott–Schottky plots of Al and E-Zn@Al. h) UPS analysis. i) Energy-band plots of Al, Zn, and E-Zn@Al. Schematic diagram of electron transfer to the LUMO level of the solvation structure.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To verify the electronic environment in the actual samples, we used Kelvin probe force microscopy (KPFM) and the Mott–Schottky technique and to examine the electron escape energy barrier and the electrical properties of the zinc matrix. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1e,f</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) show that the average interfacial potential of the initial E-Zn@Al is 50&nbsp;mV, which higher than that of the Al substrates (−125&nbsp;mV). The E-Zn@Al surface exhibited a higher electronic chemical potential than did the Al surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0013_R_d235616717e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Science">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>E</i><sub>FB</sub> for Al and E-Zn@Al can be derived from the Mott–Schottky plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1g</a>), and their highest occupied molecular orbitals (HOMO) and lowest unoccupied molecular orbitals (LUMO) energy levels are projected.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0014_R_d235616717e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to frontier molecular orbital theory, E-Zn@Al with high LUMO energy levels possesses strong reduction reactivity and is more susceptible to electron loss than Al, which is also related to the reactivity of the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0015_R_d235616717e729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, we obtain the work functions (<i>W</i><sub>f</sub>) of Zn, Al, and E-Zn@Al through ultraviolet photoelectron spectroscopy (UPS, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1h</a>). The electrochemical potential is determined by the material's work function, and a smaller <i>W</i><sub>f</sub> value indicates a closer proximity to the positive potential, thereby indicating enhanced electron provision to the electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0001">1i</a>). The <i>W</i><sub>f</sub> value of E-Zn@Al is 2.94&nbsp;eV, which is lower than those of Zn (4.28&nbsp;eV) and Al (4.33&nbsp;eV). The disparity in the work function propels the electrons to migrate from the substrate toward the substrate/electrolyte interface, thereby increasing the susceptibility of the electrolyte to undergo a reduction reaction. These results suggest that a zinc matrix with specific edge electronic harmonics could provide enough electrons to accelerate the degradation dynamics of the electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>The stable and reversible operation of batteries significantly relies on the feature of the SEI, which is determined by the selective adsorption and dissociation of ions on the substrate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0016_R_d235616717e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were used to investigate the density of states of the surface plane structure and rich edge structure (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202413253-fig-0002">2a</a>). The results indicate that the E-Zn@Al intersects with the Fermi level and is significantly more (−2.27&nbsp;eV) than Al (−7.8&nbsp;eV). In terms of conductivity, the performance of E-Zn@Al improved if other factors were not considered. The distinct peaks on the left side of E-Zn@Al can be attributed to the contribution from the D-orbital of Zn. Moreover, the wide energy gap surrounding both sides of the Fermi level in E-Zn@Al indicates a covalent nature within the E-Zn@Al system. The Fermi level of E-Zn@Al (−2.27&nbsp;eV) is higher than those of Zn (−8.12&nbsp;eV), and Al (−7.8&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0002">2b</a>). Therefore, the E-Zn@Al basis is more likely to spill electrons.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202413253-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cb6fa269-b1e7-49fa-9414-1797ad013b4e/adma202413253-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cb6fa269-b1e7-49fa-9414-1797ad013b4e/adma202413253-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ae0a9ee0-5846-4834-8362-54955f048da7/adma202413253-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cb6fa269-b1e7-49fa-9414-1797ad013b4e/adma202413253-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413253-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Zinc flower edges induced high-entropy SEI formation process. a) Density of states for the P-Zn, P-Al, and E-Zn@Al model structures. The Fermi energy is set as zero. b) <i>E</i><sub>f</sub> values of the P-Zn, P-Al, and E-Zn@Al model structures. c) Schematic diagram of the P-F orbital hybrid. d) Differential charge density distributions of the most stable configuration of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> on different facets and edges including (101), (002), (100), (100/101), (100/002), (101/101), and (101/002). e) Related data of differential charge densities. f) CV curves of Na||Al and Na||E-Zn@Al coin batteries at a sweep rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. g,h) Contour plots plotted by in situ Raman spectroscopy of Na||E-Zn@Al and Na||Al under a constant current discharge of 50&nbsp;µA cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Based on the molecular orbital theory, the valence shell electron orbital energy level diagram of the hexafluorophosphate anion can be deduced from the three principles of linear combination of atomic orbitals. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0002">2c</a>, the bond level of P-F in the hexafluorophosphate molecule at its initial state is 2. However, when additional electrons enter its molecular orbital, the bond level decreases to 1.5. At this point, there is an increase in system energy which hinders the stable existence of hexafluorophosphate. Additionally, it can be inferred from hybrid orbital theory that the initial hexafluorophosphate exhibits Sp<sup>3</sup>d<sup>2</sup> hybridization and exists in a fully filled state; any foreign electrons would disrupt molecular homeostasis. The adsorption of anions on different exposed crystal faces and crystal face boundaries was investigated via DFT calculations to evaluate the inner Helmholtz plane (IHP) structure. Three crystal faces and four interfacial boundaries including (101), (002), (100), (100/101), (100/002), (101/101), and (101/002) are considered. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0002">2d,e</a>, the adsorption strength of the anion to the facet boundary is higher than that of the other fundamental facets, indicating that higher anion concentration in IHP on facet boundary compared to the IHP on fundamental facets. The abundant anions in the IHP coupled with interfacial electron transfer quickly obtain an inorganic-rich high-entropy SEI with Na<sup>+</sup> flux and stable mechanical properties and promote uniform and dense sodium deposition on the substrate.</p>
         
         <p>The impact of multiedge structures on the SEI formation was investigated via employing cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves and in situ enhanced Raman spectroscopy. The CV data in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0002">2f</a> show that the coin cells assembled with the two kinds of substrates were tested within 2.5–0.0&nbsp;V, facilitating our understanding of the specific reduction process of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and DGM on the substrates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0017_R_d235616717e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The large response current of E-Zn@Al indicates that the electrolyte undergoes a violent reduction reaction at 0.89&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>)<sup>,</sup> with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> decomposition being the most violent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0018_R_d235616717e835" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reduction potential of 0.65&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) corresponds to the decomposition of the DGM. In addition, the Zn-Na alloying peak at 0.01&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) is located behind the SEI forming peak, suggesting that the initial SEI formation is primarily influenced primarily by the zinc morphology and electronic properties rather than the NaZn<sub>13</sub> alloy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0002">2f</a>). However, the peak of the Al substrate's DGM decomposition of the Al substrate does not appear, possibly because the Zn matrix promotes interfacial electron transfer and activates solvent decomposition. The organic and inorganic hybrid SEI can provide a high elastic modulus to adapt to the volume expansion of sodium metal deposits. Moreover, the decomposition process of the electrolyte on different substrates was investigated via in situ Raman spectroscopy under a constant current discharge of 50&nbsp;µA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0002">2g,h</a>). In the initial state, the Raman signal of the E-Zn@Al substrate at 530 cm<sup>−1</sup> is significantly stronger than that of the Al substrate, which further indicates that anions tend to accumulate on the E-Zn@Al surface. Na||E-Zn@Al exhibited significant changes at 530 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>), 1180 cm<sup>−1</sup> (CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>), and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup> (C<i>─</i>C) after a discharge of 400 s.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0019_R_d235616717e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Phys. Rev. B">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Na||Al does not significantly change even after discharge for 1350 s, indicating that the E-Zn@Al substrate can accelerate the decomposition of electrolytes to form a favorable SEI.</p>
         
         <p>The structure of the SEI structure also plays a decisive role in battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0020_R_d235616717e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cryogenic transmission electron microscopy (cryo-TEM) technique was applied to investigate the SEI structure. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202413253-fig-0003">3a</a>, the sodium metal is encapsulated by a thick and inhomogeneous SEI for Na||Al batteries. The formation of an unstable and thick SEI results in the successive loss of limited Na and, eventually, a short cycle life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0021_R_d235616717e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unlike Na||Al, a uniform and well-defined boundary line at the edge of the SEI with a thickness of ≈18&nbsp;nm in Na||E-Zn@Al is observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0003">3b</a>). High-resolution cryo-TEM revealed that the SEI layers consisted mainly of inorganic components. The main components are identified by the Fourier transform as NaF (200), Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (111), ZnF<sub>2</sub> (100), and NaZn<sub>13</sub> (400), with distributions of the corresponding lattice fringes. To investigate the origin of the zinc species in the SEI, we investigated the stability of E-Zn@Al in the electrolyte. A trace amount of 1-(2-Pyridylazo)-2-naphthol was added as a color development agent for Zn<sup>2+</sup>, and after soaking the E-Zn@Al base for 72&nbsp;h, the color of the solution changed from yellow to an orange-red (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The content of Zn<sup>2+</sup> in the electrolyte was further quantitatively analyzed via inductively coupled plasma (ICP). After 72 h of immersion, the concentration of zinc ions reached 0.04&nbsp;ppm, indicating that the NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DGM electrolyte corroded the E-Zn@Al, resulting in a minor dissolution of zinc (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the broken P<i>─</i>F bonds can combine with zinc ions to form ZnF<sub>2</sub>. The NaZn<sub>13</sub> component may be zinc alloyed with sodium, which is subsequently incorporated into the SEI. The SEI chemistry was investigated via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0003">3c</a>). Inorganic components such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (C1s, 290.1&nbsp;eV) and NaF (F1s, 684.7&nbsp;eV) are detected on the surfaces of the E-Zn@Al substrates. The rich interfacial phase of inorganic components facilitates the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> and acts as a protective barrier, preventing direct contact between the Na metal and the electrolyte to mitigate potential side reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0009_R_d235616717e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the high interfacial energy exhibited by NaF and NaCO<sub>3</sub> promotes Na<sup>+</sup> lateral migration and deposition, thereby suppressing the formation and growth of dendrites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0022_R_d235616717e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, few NaF and NaCO<sub>3</sub> are found on the Na metal anode with the Al substrate, indicating that the SEI layers comprising minimal inorganic salts are inferior.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202413253-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0fab14af-f1ef-433d-b11c-e74eac52cc49/adma202413253-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0fab14af-f1ef-433d-b11c-e74eac52cc49/adma202413253-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/14a5a01a-c221-4599-a815-38d0eba05ba8/adma202413253-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0fab14af-f1ef-433d-b11c-e74eac52cc49/adma202413253-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413253-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The structure and physicochemical properties of high-entropy SEI. a,b) Cryo-TEM characterization of the SEI on the a) Al and b) E-Zn@Al, and composition of the SEI of on E-Zn@Al via high-resolution cryo-TEM and FFT images of the lattice fringes in the SEI. c) Compositions of the SEI on two types of substrates after 3 cycles at a current of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and the corresponding ex situ XPS patterns of F 1s and C 1s spectra. d) <i>E</i><sub>a,ct</sub> values of Na||E-Zn@Al batteries and Na||Al batteries. e) <i>E</i><sub>a,SEI</sub> of Na||E-Zn@Al batteries and Na||Al batteries. f,g) Kelvin probe force microscopy images of the interfacial potentials of the Al and E-Zn@Al substrates after 3 cycles at a current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. h) Young's modulus of Al and E-Zn@Al after 3 cycles. i) Schematic illustrations of the high-entropy SEI.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In situ GC-mass spectrometry was used to examine the gas compositions produced by the cells with different current collectors during the discharge process and further elucidate the mechanism of SEI formation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The primary component of the gas produced by the batteries is H<sub>2</sub>, which is mainly derived mainly from electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0023_R_d235616717e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The H<sub>2</sub> yield in Na||E-Zn@Al (0.44&nbsp;µmol) is less than that in Na||Al (1.27&nbsp;µmol), indicating that as the reaction proceeds, the resulting SEI inhibits the continued dissolution of sodium metal in the DGM, and the cell stops producing H<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0021_R_d235616717e1006" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Temperature-dependent EIS was measured from 10 to 35&nbsp;°C to investigate the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and diffusion through the SEI. The <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) are obtained via equivalent circuit fitting. The <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of E-Zn@Al are lower than those of Al (0.25 vs 192.7 Ω for <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>; 1.19 vs 2006 Ω for <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at room temperature, possibly because the abundant inorganic components in the SEI can accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> transport at the interface and the rapid diffusion within the SEI. The activation energies of Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation and its diffusion in the SEI were calculated according to the classical Arrhenius law (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0003">3d,e</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation energy barrier (<i>E</i><sub>a,ct</sub>) and activation energy for the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (<i>E</i><sub>a, SEI</sub>) of the E-Zn@Al substrate are 21.937 and 13.998&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are lower than those for the Al substrate (58.608&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> for <i>E</i><sub>a, ct</sub> and 42.822&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> for <i>E</i><sub>a, SEI</sub>). In summary, a thin, stable and highly ionic conductive SEI is formed on the surface of the E-Zn@Al substrate during deposition, which is beneficial for the rate capability due to the short diffusion length of sodium ions. In addition, accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> transport can reduce the generation of dead sodium and sodium dendrites during plating, which is conducive to the uniform deposition of Na and improves the battery's CE.</p>
         
         <p>KPFM is applied to assess the merits of the generated SEI. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0003">3f,g</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) show that the average interfacial potential of the E-Zn@Al substrate (1295&nbsp;mV) is lower than that of the Al substrate (1470&nbsp;mV) after three cycles. This finding indicates that a uniform SEI layer is formed on the E-Zn@Al substrate to isolate it from the electrolyte and reduce the electron overflow. The ideal SEI should have good elastic strain and a large elastic modulus. We characterized the elastic modulus of the SEI using AFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0003">3h</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with the bare Al, the E-Zn@Al substrate can provide elastic strain for stress relief during sodium plating. After three cycles, the SEI on the E-Zn@Al substrate exhibited an elastic modulus of ≈40&nbsp;GPa, indicating that the Zn-induced SEI possesses remarkable toughness to mitigate the volume expansion of the sodium metal layer under high areal capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0024_R_d235616717e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This finding is consistent with the results of the XPS analysis. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0003">3i</a>, the high-entropy SEI has a fast and uniform sodium ion transport channel, resulting in ordered sodium deposition.</p>
         
         <p>Sodium deposition involves Na<sup>+</sup> transfer from the electrolyte to metallic sodium, during which the electron transfer, solvation, and Na<sup>+</sup> transfer is hindered by the SEI. Here, the interfacial exchange current density (<i>J</i><sub>0</sub>) is used to reflect the intrinsic dynamics of electron transfer coupling to the solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> transfer (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4a</a>). <i>J</i><sub>0</sub> may be the key factor that determines the electrochemistry of Na<sup>+</sup> exchange. EIS analysis was employed to measure <i>J</i><sub>0</sub> in the asymmetric batteries. A comparison of the EIS data of different batteries reveals that the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> values of the SEI and <i>J</i><sub>0</sub> exhibit inverse correlations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4b,c</a>). The electrochemical data suggest that a higher <i>J</i><sub>0</sub> leads to a higher CE in batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202413253-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/67c7a657-9fac-4060-b880-afbc2dc0d29b/adma202413253-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/67c7a657-9fac-4060-b880-afbc2dc0d29b/adma202413253-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f90f9d1f-9073-47d6-8975-07046237877b/adma202413253-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/67c7a657-9fac-4060-b880-afbc2dc0d29b/adma202413253-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413253-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium deposition morphology evolution and mechanism with high-entropy SEI. a) Schematic diagram of the relationship between the interfacial exchange current density <i>J</i><sub>0</sub> and the impedance of each part. b) AC impedance spectra for the Na metal anodes with E-Zn@Al and bare Al in the first two cycles. c) Interfacial exchange current density and “<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>” of Na metal anodes with E-Zn@Al and bare Al during the first two cycles. d) Relationship among the nucleation barrier Δ<i>G</i><sub>crit</sub>, critical nucleus size (<i>r</i><sup>*</sup>) and interface exchange current density <i>J</i><sub>0</sub>. e–g) SEM images of Na plated on the E-Zn@Al and Al substrates at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. The scale is 1&nbsp;µm. h) Thickness data of Na deposited on E-Zn@Al and Al substrates with different areal capacity at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. i) Comparisons of the number density of nuclei between the E-Zn@Al and Al substrates. j) Adsorption free energies of Na atoms on the E-Zn@Al, NaZn13, Al, and various facets of Na metal.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">Classical nucleation theory (CNT) further verifies the correctness of the inference. CNT is first used in a condensate gas system and is extended to the liquid–solid balance system of the saturated solution crystallization phenomenon, which is based on the interface between the clusters; the surrounding environment can turn into a nuclear power base.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0025_R_d235616717e1181" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Lett. Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to CNT, the difference in chemical potential is the driving force for the nucleation of saturated vapor. For electrochemical deposition, the surface overpotential (|<i>η</i>|) (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)) is the overpotential in Butler–Volmer equation (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>)) for a given current density and is the driving force for the electrochemical surface nucleation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0026_R_d235616717e1195" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Joule">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relationship between the exchange current density and the surface overpotential (|<i>η</i>|) (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>)) is obtained from the relationship between the exchange current density (<i>J</i>) and the current density (Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and see Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) for the meaning of the letters in the equations)

                                       <mi>C</mi>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                                       <mo>∗</mo>
                                    </msubsup>
                                 </mfrac>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>e</mi>
                                    <mfenced close="]" open="[" separators="">
                                       <mfrac>
                                          <mrow>
                                             <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                                <mrow>
                                                   <mn>1</mn>

                                             </mfenced>

                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\rm{J}} = {{{\rm{J}}}_0}{\rm{\;}}\left\{ {{{{{C}_{\rm{O}}}} \over {C_{\rm{O}}^{\rm{*}}}}{{e}^{\left( {{{ - \alpha F{\rm{\eta }}} \over {RT}}} \right)}} - {{{{C}_{\rm{O}}}} \over {C_{\rm{O}}^{\rm{*}}}}{{e}^{\left[ {{{\left( {1 - \alpha } \right)F{\rm{\eta }}} \over {RT}}} \right]}}} \right\}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The critical nuclear size (<i>r</i><sup>*</sup>) can be derived by applying the 3D sphere-forming kernel model of the smallest cluster to Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>) (Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Its relationship with <i>J</i> (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a>)) is derived from Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The critical nucleation barrier (Δ<i>G<sub>r</sub></i><sub>*</sub>) is the energy barrier to overcome to start nucleation, and its expression can be derived accordingly (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>); and see Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> for derivation, Supporting Information)

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202413253-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
