         </div>
         
         <p>The relationship between the Δ<i>Gr</i>* (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0007" title="Link to equation">7</a>)) and <i>J</i> is obtained from Equation (<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, and Note <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which corresponds to the remaining energy to overcome the cluster interface energy. The Δ<i>Gr</i><sup>*</sup> is inversely proportional to <i>J</i><sup>2</sup>. The interfacial exchange current density <i>j</i><sub>0</sub> and the critical nuclear size <i>r</i><sup>*</sup> are proposed. According to the above theory, the larger the interfacial exchange current density is, the smaller the nuclear size of sodium plating is, and the more uniform and compact the sodium plating is (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4d</a>).</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The interface exchange current density is also known as the pseudo current density, where the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> is obtained by the sum of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>. The calculation (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adma202413253-disp-0008" title="Link to equation">8</a>)) of the interface exchange current density is as follows

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adma202413253-disp-0008"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6193c987-2106-480a-a905-83634153196b/adma202413253-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202413253:adma202413253-math-0008" display="block" location="graphic/adma202413253-math-0008.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>J</mi>
                              <mn>0</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mfrac>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mi>T</mi>
                              </mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>n</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>e</mi>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mo>′</mo>
                                       <mo>′</mo>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </msup>
                                 <msup>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>R</mi>
                                       <mi>SEI</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mo>′</mo>
                                       <mo>′</mo>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                           </mfrac>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{J_0} = \frac{{kT}}{{ne^{\prime\prime}{R_{{\mathrm{SEI}}}}^{\prime \prime }}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(8)</span></div>where <i>k</i> is the Boltzmann constant, <i>T</i> is the temperature of the system (here, <i>T</i> = 297 K), <i>n</i> = 1 is the charge carried by each ion (for Na<sup>+</sup>), and <i>e</i> is the basic charge.</div>
         
         <p>Uniform and dense sodium deposition is a key factor for high-performance AFSMBs. We studied the morphology evolution process of sodium nucleation and growth via SEM at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4e,f</a>). The sodium nuclei are distributed sparsely on the Al substrate and grow like islands (1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>). This outcome may be due to the uneven Na flux of the SEI and the preferential growth of sodium in the high-energy grain boundaries of the substrate. The further deposition of Na (3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>) results in many cracks and filamentous structures in the deposited layer. In contrast, sodium nuclei are uniformly and densely distributed on the E-Zn@Al substrate surface and have a spherical structure. This finding suggests that the SEI with a low ion diffusion barrier can provide uniform Na<sup>+</sup> flux to the substrate, and that the Zn matrix can act as nucleation sites. Further Na deposition results in the gradual diffusion of the spherical sodium nuclei to form a smooth-surface sodium deposit. Sodium is deposited under different capacities ranging from 1 to 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> to understand the thickness of Na deposits in different substrates. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4g</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) show the cross-sectional SEM images of the deposited Na, where the Na deposit demonstrates different thicknesses on the E-Zn@Al and Al substrates, particularly at 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. In cross-sectional SEM determines that Na is deposited on the surface of the zinc layer on the E-Zn@Al substrate rather than in the interlayer of Zn and Al. The thickness of the Na layer on the E-Zn@Al substrate is 9, 18, and 22&nbsp;µm at 1, 2, and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. The thicknesses of the Na layer on the Al substrate are 21 and 35&nbsp;µm at 2 and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, respectively. The thickness of the sodium layer is not uniform at a low capacity and cannot be measured correctly because of the sodiophilic nature of the Al substrate. The theoretical thicknesses of 1, 2, and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> Na are 6.7, 13, and 20&nbsp;µm, respectively, at zero porosity. We summarized the thickness data of the sodium coating under different capacities when the capacity increased (1–3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4h</a>). The data show that the thickness of the sodium layer on the E-Zn@Al substrate is lower than that on the Al substrate at any capacity and is close to the theoretical value. The possible reason is that the use of the E-Zn@Al substrate greatly reduces the initial porosity of Na and increases the uniform nucleation sites, and the spherical growth of sodium makes the Na layer smooth and dense.</p>
         
         <p>The nucleation and growth of Na on different substrates were further confirmed by chronoamperometry (CA) analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4i</a>). Regardless of the substrate, sodium deposition shows an instantaneous nucleation pattern. We concluded through the CA data analysis that the nucleation number density on the E-Zn@Al substrate (7.36 × 10<sup>13</sup> N cm<sup>−2</sup>) is much higher than that on the Al substrate (2.08 × 10<sup>13</sup> N cm<sup>−2</sup>), which corresponds to the SEM data. The free energy of adsorption was calculated and compared to understand the internal cause of the homogeneous deposition of Na on the E-Zn@Al substrate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0004">4j</a>). The adsorption-free energy of sodium atoms on E-Zn@Al is much higher than that on the Al substrate and its different crystal faces. This outcome suggests that the E-Zn@Al substrate is in the Frank–van der Merwe growth mode. These results show that uniform Na<sup>+</sup> flux and favorable nucleation sites can induce a uniform and dense sodium deposition morphology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e1691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The quantitative evaluation of the impact of the zinc matrix on the kinetics of Na deposition was conducted by determining the activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) through the Arrhenius equation. The Nyquist plots of asymmetric batteries with E-Zn@Al or Al at different temperatures are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a–c</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). The <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> value can be obtained via linear fitting of the transfer charge resistance versus temperature. The E-Zn@Al substrate shows a low <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> value of 33.02&nbsp;kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, chronoamperometry was performed at −50&nbsp;mV to assess the effect of the zinc matrix on the Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na+</sub>). The <i>t</i><sub>Na+</sub> of the E-Zn@Al substrate reaches 0.55, which is higher than that of Al (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15d–f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, the E-Zn@Al substrate is superior to the pure Al substrate in enhancing electrode kinetics.</p>
         
         <p>The CV (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202413253-fig-0005">5a</a>) curves at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> further confirm that the Na metal anodes with the E-Zn@Al substrate exhibit a stronger response current density and a smaller deposition overpotential than those with bare Al. This finding indicates that the sodium on the zinc surface has a low nuclear barrier and high reversibility. In addition, the cycle life and ACE of electroplating stripping are important parameters for investigating the reversibility of E-Zn@Al. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a,b</a> (Supporting Information) shows that an average CE of above 99.90% with a low Na plating overpotential (&lt;20&nbsp;mV) is achieved for all the current densities of 1 and 2&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> after 1100 cycles (2200&nbsp;h). When the areal capacity is increased to 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, Na plating/stripping on E-Zn@Al substrates exhibit remarkable durability, sustaining 1000 cycles with an average CE of 99.95% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0005">5b,c</a>). However, it fails to function under identical cycling conditions in Na||Al batteries. Moreover, the constant current voltage and capacity curves of the Na||E-Zn@Al battery remain stable with different numbers of cycles at a current density of 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. We repeated three sets of asymmetric battery data to ensure the reliability of the data and further verify the reversibility of the E-Zn@Al substrate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16c</a>, Supporting Information). The CE is maintained above 99.80%, and the error range is less than 0.04%, indicating that the E-Zn@Al anode sample has good consistency. Afterward, we employed symmetrical batteries to assess the efficacy of the modified interface in suppressing dendrite formation/growth. At current densities of 1, 2, and 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, E-Zn@A-Na||Na-E-Zn@Al symmetric batteries demonstrate long-term cycling stability (over 1000&nbsp;h) with low overpotentials (12, 14, and 19&nbsp;mV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0005">5d</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d</a>, Supporting Information). However, the Al-Na||Na-Al and Na||Na symmetric batteries show increased polarization and short circuits shortly after sodium plating/stripping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a–c</a>, Supporting Information). The E-Zn@Al substrate demonstrates better performance than the currently reported anode-free substrates, particularly with regard to ACE and cycle life (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0005">5e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0005_R_d235616717e1777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e1777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a>, <a href="#adma202413253-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0027_R_d235616717e1777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater. Nano Lett. Small">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202413253-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3f315554-3101-486e-9647-2d1ff53a0b3d/adma202413253-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3f315554-3101-486e-9647-2d1ff53a0b3d/adma202413253-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ecb84a26-b4ac-45a4-8433-d77b35e4ed33/adma202413253-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3f315554-3101-486e-9647-2d1ff53a0b3d/adma202413253-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413253-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the half batteries. a) CV curves of E-Zn@Al and bare Al cycling at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (illustration locally enlarged). b) Galvanostatic potential versus capacity curves of the batteries at 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Comparison of the CEs of Na plating/stripping on Al and E-Zn@Al substrates at current densities of 1, 2, and 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1, 2, and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. d) Voltage versus time curves of Na-E-Zn@Al symmetrical batteries at different current densities. e) Comparison of average Coulombic efficiency, cycle life, and cumulative areal capacity with different substrates. f) Titration gas chromatography and optical images of H<sub>2</sub> after H<sub>2</sub>O titration on substrates from the asymmetrical battery after 10 cycles at a current density of 3&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. g,h) The ex situ electrochemical impedance spectra curves of the asymmetric battery g) in the initial state and h) after the first cycle. The illustration shows the corresponding <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> values.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Na metal anodes with the E-Zn@Al substrates exhibited high reversibility during plating/stripping. The residual Na content of the two substrates was determined by gas chromatography (GC) and water titration after 10 sodium plating/stripping cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0005">5f</a>). The H<sub>2</sub> emission from E-Zn@Al is significantly lower than that from bare Al, indicating that E-Zn@Al has minimal dead sodium. This finding further confirms that the Zn matrix can enhance the reversibility of sodium plating/stripping. The optical images of 0.1 and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> sodium metal plating and stripping on different substrates show that the sodium metal is uniformly deposited and almost completely stripped on the E-Zn@Al material. The inhibition of dendrite growth on the E-Zn@Al substrates was investigated through in situ optical microscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17e</a>, Supporting Information). After an initial 10&nbsp;min of deposition, the bare Al foils exhibited a nonuniform Na morphology with certain protrusions. These protuberances persist and develop into needlelike dendrites during subsequent deposition. In contrast, the deposition of Na on E-Zn@Al appears smooth. In situ XRD can be used to evaluate the role of the Zn interface in optimizing sodium deposition, thereby characterizing the structural evolution of sodium on different substrates in asymmetric batteries (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17f,g</a>, Supporting Information). During plating, the peak intensity near 29.4° can be attributed to the (110) lattice of the Na metal. A residual Na signal exists when bare Al is used at the end of stripping, whereas the peak for the Na metal with the E-Zn@Al substrate disappears. This finding indicates that the Zn interface exhibits a highly reversible Na plating/stripping. The color change of E-Zn@Al can be clearly observed in the optical picture of Na plating/stripping. We employed XRD to analyze the its component. According to the XRD pattern in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), the peaks of E-Zn@Al match well with the diffraction patterns of NaZn<sub>13</sub> (PDF#03-1008) and P-O-Zn (PDF#01-0966), respectively. The compound NaZn<sub>13</sub> is a binary alloy composed of sodium and zinc during platting process. For P-O-Zn, this may be due to the reaction between ZnO and the decomposition products of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>. The black color of E-Zn@Al after cycling may be due to the presence of black P-O-Zn. The EIS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0005">5g,h</a>, Supporting Information) revealed the impedance changes in the asymmetric batteries with different substrates during the initial charging and discharging process. The <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> of the cell with bare Al is approximately twice that of the cell with E-Zn@Al after cycling, revealing that the Na metal anode with E-Zn@Al exhibits a thin and uniform SEI. Thus, the resistance of the electrons passing through the SEI is reduced, and the reaction kinetics are enhanced. According to the experimental data and analysis in the chapter, the E-Zn@Al substrate is located in the dendrite-free and highly reversible part of the quadrant plot, which is an ideal state to reduce sodium loss.</p>
         
         <p>The full batteries are assembled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.85</sub>Mn<sub>0.15</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFMPP), and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>4</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>5</sub>·xH<sub>2</sub>O (NFPP) as cathodes to evaluate the potential application of the E-Zn@Al substrate. E-Zn@Al||NVP full batteries (ultrahigh loading of 32&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) exhibit a reversible capacity of 103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (3.74&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) with a high capacity retention of 86% after 90 cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202413253-fig-0006">6a</a>). The E-Zn@Al||NVP batteries deliver a high-energy density of 353&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Further analysis of the AFSMBs confirmed the important role of the zinc matrix (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0006">6b</a>). AFSMBs exhibit a high average energy efficiency of 95% with a stable median voltage, whereas Al||NVP full batteries fail to operate properly. After 90 cycles, the polarization voltage of the charge and discharge curve is ≈90&nbsp;mV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0006">6c</a>). After 90 cycles, the battery is disassembled for SEM analysis to verify the reversibility of E-Zn@Al. The results show that the morphology of the zinc matrix is almost unchanged, suggesting inhibited sodium loss and dendrite growth. Moreover, when the current density was further increased to 1 C (37.4&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), the cycling performance of the E-Zn@Al||NVP batteries is reliable for 200 cycles with the capacity retention of 86.8% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202413253-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a35ec452-67be-4d20-90f0-07802d660c9e/adma202413253-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a35ec452-67be-4d20-90f0-07802d660c9e/adma202413253-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/29df2e50-eaa5-4fb2-aa9a-7895e7e0e998/adma202413253-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a35ec452-67be-4d20-90f0-07802d660c9e/adma202413253-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413253-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413253" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the full batteries. a) Cyclic stability of AFSMBs (E-Zn@Al||NVP) between 2.5 and 3.8&nbsp;V at a current rate of 0.1 C. b) Energy efficiency and median voltage of E-Zn@Al ||NVP AFSMBs. c) Charge–discharge curve and SEM image of E-Zn@Al after 90 cycles. d) Cycling performance of anode less E-Zn@Al-Na||NVP single layer pouch batteries (N/P = 1.5, 2, 3) and Al-Na||NVP single layer pouch batteries with high loading (10 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) at 0.1C. (1 C = 117 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). e) Cyclic performance and discharge/charge curves of E-Zn@Al-Na||NFMPP single layer pouch batteries at 1C (N/P = 1.5). f) Ragone plot of the AF/LSMBs as compared with recent reports, where energy densities are evaluated on the basis of the active material loading of the anodes and cathodes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, commercial application prospects under harsh conditions, including low N/P ratios (N/P = 3, 2, and 1.5) and high cathode loading (10 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>) have been investigated via single-layer pouch batteries. The E-Zn@Al-Na||NVP full batteries exhibit superior electrochemical performance, with the capacity remaining at 96% of the first cycle capacity after 340 cycles at a current rate of 0.1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0006">6d</a>; 1 C = 117&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>); the GCD curves corresponding to the first 100 cycles of cycling show negligible capacity decay, with a redox plateau of ≈3.4&nbsp;V for the NVP compared with Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, after 10 cycles of cycling of the Al-Na||NVP full batteries (N/P&nbsp;= 2), the capacity decays rapidly, the CE is quite low, and the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves corresponding to the first 10 cycles show irreversible capacity fading (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20c</a> (Supporting Information), the E-Zn@Al-Na||NVP full batteries (N/P&nbsp;= 2) display 97% capacity retention (≈103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup><sub>NVP</sub>) after 250 cycles at 0.5 C. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the capacity of the batteries are 104.44 and 86.40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 and 10 C, respectively. The corresponding GCD curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a>, Supporting Information) show a small capacity change at high current density, and the rate performance is compared with that of previously reported SIBs or SMBs, which deliver superior performance, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The E-Zn@Al-Na||NFMPP full batteries (N/P&nbsp;= 1.5) demonstrate an ultralong cycle life of 600 cycles at 1 C with a capacity retention of 99% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0006">6e</a>). The E-Zn@Al-Na||NFPP full cells (N/P = 2) also exhibited stable long-term cycling performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21d</a>, Supporting Information). The enhanced cycling performance and superior reversibility of the E-Zn@Al based full cells can be attributed to the robust SEI and the reduced Na consumption. The energy densities of the full batteries are calculated based on the total mass of the sodium metal deposited on the substrate (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), which delivers the energy densities of 295.35&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (N/P = 1.5), 285.92&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (N/P = 2), and 262.32&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (N/P = 3). Comparisons are made with previously reported SIBs or SMBs, and Ragone plots are plotted for comparison (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413253-fig-0006">6f</a>; and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). These performances are significantly better than the performances from previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413253-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0008_R_d235616717e2013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Small Methods Nanoscale Nano Energy Nano Energy Energy Environ. Sci. Energy Environ. Sci. Matter Adv. Mater. Sci. Adv. J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. eScience">8</a>, <a href="#adma202413253-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0009_R_d235616717e2013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#adma202413253-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413253-bib-0028_R_d235616717e2013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Commun. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nat. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enhanced performance can be attributed to the E-Zn@Al substrate, which can effectively reduce the loss of active Na ions from the cathode because of its embitterment reversibility. In addition, the E-Zn@Al substrate-based AFSMBs with high-loading NVP cathode can dramatically enhance the energy density of the batteries. They are much superior to conventional SMBs with low active material loading. Furthermore, the scalable roll-to-roll technique applied in this research features a simple production method, low material cost, and environmentally friendly aqueous solvents. This technique is promising for commercialization with large-scale production and desirable reproducibility.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202413253-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202413253-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, a high-entropy SEI with discrete multicomponent inorganics (ZnF<sub>2</sub>, NaZn<sub>13,</sub> NaF, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>) is successfully developed to enable high cathode loading in AFSMBs through the specific facet boundary and the electron-donating property of zinc flower edges. Compared with the plane Al substrate, the exposed edge sites between the (100) and (101) facets in flower-zinc facilitate anion adsorption, directing a greater number of anions to preferentially participate in the IHP. Simultaneously, electron delocalization at the edges expedites electron transfer to the electrolyte and electron injection improves the dissociation kinetics of the electrolyte. The distinctive IHP, efficient electron transfer and SEI homogeneous-nucleating template collectively contribute to the rapid formation of a thin and inorganic-rich SEI layer with a high-entropy discrete multicomponent distribution. The SEI can provide high-flux Na ion transport field, thereby reducing the critical nucleation barrier, and promoting high density sodium nucleation and pyknotic growth (7.36 × 10<sup>13</sup> N cm<sup>−2</sup>, 22&nbsp;µm) and boosting the cycle life of the AFSMBs. Classical nucleation theory and the Butler–Volmer equation also validate the proposed Na growth model with this SEI, elucidating a positive correlation between the dynamics of Na ion transfer and the nucleation radius. Asymmetric batteries exhibit high reversibility, with a CE of 99.95% and a long cycle life of over 1000 cycles at 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The assembled anode-free batteries exhibit optimized cyclic stability with ultrahigh initial Coulombic efficiency (97.05%), even with a high cathode loading of 32&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Anode-less single layer pouch batteries can realize impressive cycling stability with 99% capacity retention after 600 cycles. The insight of this high-entropy interface strategy provides a new way to improve the interface ion transport field and pyknotic metal deposits for performance improvement in anode-free batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
